        <meta property="og:url" content="https://pubs.acs.org/doi/full/10.1021/acsami.6b09496"><meta property="og:title" content="3D-0D Graphene-Fe3O4 Quantum Dot Hybrids as High-Performance Anode Materials for Sodium-Ion Batteries"><meta property="og:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b09496/asset/images/acsami.6b09496.social.jpeg_v03"><meta property="og:description" content="Transition metal oxides can be considered as appealing candidates for sodium ion battery anode materials because these low-cost materials possess high capacity and enhanced safety. However, the practical application of these materials is usually limited by their low electronic conductivity and serious volume change during the charging–discharging process. Herein, we report the fabrication of 3D-0D graphene-Fe3O4 quantum dot hybrids by a facile one-pot hydrothermal approach as anode materials for sodium-ion batteries. Fe3O4 quantum dots with an average size of 4.9 nm are anchored on the surface of 3D structured graphene nanosheets homogeneously. Such unique hierarchical structure are advantageous for enlarging the electrode/electrolyte interface area and enhancing the electrochemical activity of the hybrid materials, inhibiting particle aggregation of Fe3O4 and accommodating their volume change during the charging–discharging process as well as enabling fast diffusion of electrons and rapid transfer of electrolyte ions. Consequently, the 3D-0D graphene-Fe3O4 quantum dot hybrids exhibit ultrahigh sodium storage capacity (525 mAh g–1 at 30 mA g–1), outstanding cycling stability (312 mAh g–1 after 200 cycles at 50 mA g–1) and superior rate performance (56 mAh g–1 at 10 A g–1)."><meta property="og:type" content="Article"><meta property="og:site_name" content="ACS Publications"><meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image"><meta name="twitter:site" content="@ACSPublications"><meta name="twitter:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b09496/asset/images/acsami.6b09496.social.jpeg_v03">

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/wro/nwyw~thiss-ds.js" async="" nonce="90f6e7a1cb39775c-SJC"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="90f6e7a1cb39775c-SJC">

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            timeout: 5000,

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
            },
            retrieveEntity: function (ms) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                if (ms == undefined || ms === '') {
                    ms = this.timeout;
                }

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res.data);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('failed to retrieve entity from local storage: ' + err);
                        });
                    // Set up the timeout
                    setTimeout(function () {
                        reject('Promise timed out ' + ms + ' ms');
                    }, ms);
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage due to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {
                            doi: doi,
                            entityid: entityId,
                            entityTitle: entityTitle,
                            eventGroupKey: '9210c6be-b3af-403a-8e00-bb047748917a'
                        }

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    }).fail(function (status) {
                        return reject(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (i = 0; i < allSsoInstitutions.length; i++) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        if(allSsoInstitutions.length === 0 && true){
                            self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, null, null)
                                .then(function () {
                                    console.log('No Entity found');
                                }, function (err) {
                                    console.log('failed to get the none entity status' ,err);
                                });
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            }).catch(function (err) {
                                reject(err);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();

                    if ($('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').length > 0) {
                        let institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox = $('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').get(0);
                        if (!$(institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox).is(":checked")) {
                            return;
                        }
                    }

                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="90f6e7a1cb39775c-SJC">
var dfpReady = false;
$(document).ready(function() {
    // var journalTitle = 'applied_materials_interfaces';
    var taxonomyKeywords = $('.article_header-taxonomy');
    var articleKeyword = $('meta[name="keywords"]');
    var articleTitle = $('meta[name="dc.Title"]');
    var articleDescription = $('meta[name="dc.Description"]');
    var abstractKeyword = $('p.articleBody_abstractText:contains("Keywords: ")');
    var searchTerms = [];

    if (taxonomyKeywords.length > 0) {
        $(taxonomyKeywords).children(".rlist--inline").children("li").each(function() {
            searchTerms.push($(this).text());
        });
    }
    else if (articleKeyword.length > 0) { 
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleKeyword.prop('content').split(', '));
    }
    else if (abstractKeyword.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(abstractKeyword.text().split("Keywords: ")[1].split('; '));
    }
    else if (articleDescription.length > 0) {
        $(articleDescription).each(function() {
            articleDescriptionKeywords = $(this).prop('content');
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/.,/g,"");
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/\s{2,}/g," ");
            searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleDescriptionKeywords.split(' '));
        });
    }

    if (articleTitle.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleTitle.prop('content').split(' '));
    }

    googletag.cmd.push(function() {
        var mappingLeader = googletag.sizeMapping().
        addSize([750, 200], [728, 90]). 
        addSize([0, 0], [320, 50]).
        build();
        //leaderboard
        googletag.defineSlot('/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article', [728, 90], 'dfp-journal-article-leaderboard').defineSizeMapping(mappingLeader).setTargeting('location', 'leaderboard1').setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //recommend
        googletag.defineSlot("/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article", [1, 1], "dfp-journal-article-recommend").setTargeting("ntvPlacement", ["1100167"]).setTargeting("location", "recommend").setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //googletag.pubads().enableSingleRequest();
        googletag.pubads().collapseEmptyDivs();
        googletag.pubads().setTargeting('inst',dartInstitutionType).setTargeting('memb',dartMember);
        googletag.pubads().addEventListener("slotRenderEnded", function(event) {
            console.log(event.slot.getSlotElementId() + " isEmpty: " + event.isEmpty + " | creative id: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            if((event.isEmpty === false) && (event.slot.getSlotElementId() != "dfp-journal-article-recommend")) {
                $("#" + event.slot.getSlotElementId()).parents(".advertisement").slideDown();
                console.log("ad display: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            }
            // else {
            //     console.log("no ad found");
            // }
        });
        googletag.enableServices();
        dfpReady = true;
    });
});
</script>

<script nonce="90f6e7a1cb39775c-SJC">
$(function() {
    var serialCode = "aamick";
    var books = ["reagents", "symposium", "advances", "medichemreviews", "styleguide"]
    if(books.includes(serialCode) === false) {
        // default click action
        $("[data-id]").on("mousedown", function(e) {
            var currentClickID = $(this).attr("data-id");

            if(currentClickID.includes("_hover") === false) {
                if(currentClickID.match("^article_") && !currentClickID.match("^article_abstract_CC")) {   
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "3D-0D Graphene-Fe&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt;O&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt; Quantum Dot Hybrids as High-Performance Anode Materials for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.6b09496", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                }
            }
        });
        
        // Access Management icons hover
        var articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        var articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent === false) {
                articleAccessManagementIconHover = true;
                var articleAccessManagementHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleAccessManagementIconHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "3D-0D Graphene-Fe&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt;O&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt; Quantum Dot Hybrids as High-Performance Anode Materials for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.6b09496", "Article_Element": articleAccessManagementHover });
    
                        articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        });
    
        // articleRightsPermissionsLink hover
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent === false) {
                articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = true;
                var articleRightsHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "3D-0D Graphene-Fe&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt;O&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt; Quantum Dot Hybrids as High-Performance Anode Materials for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.6b09496", "Article_Element": articleRightsHover });
    
                        articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        });
    
        // Author hover
        var articleAuthorHover = false
        var articleAuthorHoverEvent = false
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if((articleAuthorHoverEvent === false) && ($(this).parent().hasClass("view-affs") === false)) {
                articleAuthorHover = true;
                var authorInfoHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAuthorHover === true) && (articleAuthorHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "3D-0D Graphene-Fe&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt;O&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt; Quantum Dot Hybrids as High-Performance Anode Materials for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.6b09496", "Article_Element": authorInfoHover });
    
                        articleAuthorHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAuthorHover = false;
        });
    
        // CC License hover
        var articleAbstractCCHover = false
        var articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = false
        var articleDrawerCCHover = false
        var articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = false
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if($(this).attr("data-id")) {
                if(($(this).attr("data-id") === "article_drawer_CC-License") && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleDrawerCCHover = true;
                    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleDrawerCCHover === true) && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "3D-0D Graphene-Fe&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt;O&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt; Quantum Dot Hybrids as High-Performance Anode Materials for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.6b09496", "Article_Element": "article_drawer_CC-License_hover" });
    
                            articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
                else if(($(this).attr("data-id").match("^article_abstract_CC")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleAbstractCCHover = true;
    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "3D-0D Graphene-Fe&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt;O&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt; Quantum Dot Hybrids as High-Performance Anode Materials for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.6b09496", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                            articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
            }
            else if(($(this).hasClass("article__cc-license__link")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = true;
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "3D-0D Graphene-Fe&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt;O&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt; Quantum Dot Hybrids as High-Performance Anode Materials for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.6b09496", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                        articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            if(articleAbstractCCHover === true) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = false;
            }
            if(articleDrawerCCHover = true) {
                articleDrawerCCHover = false;
            }
        });
        
        // show recommeneded click delay
        var intervalCount = 0;
        var showRecommendedInterval = setInterval(function() {
            intervalCount += 100;
            if($(".show-recommended__wrapper .show-recommended").length > 0) {
                clearInterval(showRecommendedInterval);
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowMore']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "3D-0D Graphene-Fe&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt;O&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt; Quantum Dot Hybrids as High-Performance Anode Materials for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.6b09496", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowLess']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "3D-0D Graphene-Fe&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt;O&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt; Quantum Dot Hybrids as High-Performance Anode Materials for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.6b09496", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
            }
        }, 100);
    }
});
</script>

href="/action/doSearch?field1=Contrib&amp;text1=Mengqiu++Jia" data-id="article_author_search">Mengqiu Jia</a></span><span class="author-xref-symbol "><sup>*</sup></span><span class="author-xref-symbol class="articleHeaderDropzone4" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone4"><div class="articleRightsPermissionsLink"><a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D26878%26pageCount%3D8%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DHuan%2BLiu%252C%2BMengqiu%2BJia%252C%2BQizhen%2BZhu%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D8%26issueNum%3D40%26contentID%3Dacsami.6b09496%26title%3D3D-0D%2BGraphene-Fe3O4%2BQuantum%2BDot%2BHybrids%2Bas%2BHigh-Performance%2BAnode%2BMaterials%2Bfor%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%26numPages%3D8%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D26885%26publicationDate%3DOctober%2B2016" Empty dropzone --></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="f_tgr1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b09496/asset/images/medium/am-2016-09496z_0006.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b09496/asset/images/large/am-2016-09496z_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.6b09496&amp;id=f_tgr1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">Transition metal oxides can be considered as appealing candidates for sodium ion battery anode materials because these low-cost materials possess high capacity and enhanced safety. However, the practical application of these materials is usually limited by their low electronic conductivity and serious volume change during the charging–discharging process. Herein, we report the fabrication of 3D-0D graphene-Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> quantum dot hybrids by a facile one-pot hydrothermal approach as anode materials for sodium-ion batteries. Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> quantum dots with an average size of 4.9 nm are anchored on the surface of 3D structured graphene nanosheets homogeneously. Such unique hierarchical structure are advantageous for enlarging the electrode/electrolyte interface area and enhancing the electrochemical activity of the hybrid materials, inhibiting particle aggregation of Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> and accommodating their volume change during the charging–discharging process as well as enabling fast diffusion of electrons and rapid transfer of electrolyte ions. Consequently, the 3D-0D graphene-Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> quantum dot hybrids exhibit ultrahigh sodium storage capacity (525 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 30 mA g<sup>–1</sup>), outstanding cycling stability (312 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after 200 cycles at 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup>) and superior rate performance (56 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 10 A g<sup>–1</sup>).</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D26878%26pageCount%3D8%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DHuan%2BLiu%252C%2BMengqiu%2BJia%252C%2BQizhen%2BZhu%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D8%26issueNum%3D40%26contentID%3Dacsami.6b09496%26title%3D3D-0D%2BGraphene-Fe3O4%2BQuantum%2BDot%2BHybrids%2Bas%2BHigh-Performance%2BAnode%2BMaterials%2Bfor%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%26numPages%3D8%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D26885%26publicationDate%3DOctober%2B2016" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2016 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291517&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Batteries" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Batteries" class="article__tags__link">Batteries</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Electrodes" class="article__tags__link">Electrodes</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291975&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Quantum dots" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Quantum%20dots" class="article__tags__link">Quantum dots</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291251&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Sodium" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Sodium" class="article__tags__link">Sodium</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292522&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Two dimensional materials" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Two%20dimensional%20materials" class="article__tags__link">Two dimensional materials</a></li></ul></div></div></div></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--keywords hlFld-Abstract"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">
                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium-ion+Batteries&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium-ion%20batteries">sodium-ion batteries</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Fe3o4&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_Fe3O4">Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub></a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Quantum+Dots&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_quantum%20dots">quantum dots</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Graphene&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_graphene">graphene</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Three-dimensional+Structure&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_three-dimensional%20structure">three-dimensional structure</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i1">1 Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">With the imminent exhaustion of nonrenewable fossil fuels and the ever increasing problem of CO<sub>2</sub> emissions, sustainable and renewable energy sources like wind power and solar energies have attracted intense interests. An efficient, inexpensive, large-scale energy storage system is indispensable to integrate these intermittent and fluctuant energies into the electricity grid system smoothly. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1" aria-label="reference 1">(1)</a> Currently lithium ion batteries (LIBs) are commonly accepted as one of the most promising technology for their long lifetime and high energy density, but the limited storage of the Li resources which are not evenly distributed in the earth’s crust will be the restriction on their future use in large-scale electrical energy storage system. Accordingly, as the most promising alternative for large-scale energy storage, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have received renewed interest owing to their low cost as well as widespread availability of sodium resources. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4" aria-label="reference 1 to 4">(1-4)</a> However, because the sodium ion has the larger radius (0.102 nm) than the lithium ion (0.076 nm), it is a more arduous challenge to find the host materials allowing for the reversible and rapid sodium ion insertion/extraction. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref5'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref5" aria-label="reference 5">(5)</a> Especially for the anodes, the serious volume expansion and sluggish kinetics during charging–discharging process probably cause the most important issue for the host material development. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref6 ref7 ref8 ref9 ref10'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6 ref7 ref8 ref9 ref10" aria-label="reference 6 to 10">(6-10)</a> It has been demonstrated that graphite materials, the anode materials used most commonly in LIBs, are not suitable for SIBs because of their poor sodium storage capacity. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref5'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref5" aria-label="reference 5">(5)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">So far, many kinds of anode materials have been proposed such as carbonaceous materials, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref11 ref12 ref13 ref14'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref11 ref12 ref13 ref14" aria-label="reference 11 to 14">(11-14)</a> alloys, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref15 ref16'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15 ref16" aria-label="reference 15 and 16">(15, 16)</a> metal oxides/sulfates, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref17 ref18'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17 ref18" aria-label="reference 17 and 18">(17, 18)</a> and phosphorous. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref19 ref20'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19 ref20" aria-label="reference 19 and 20">(19, 20)</a> Amorphous hard carbons prepared from hydrocarbons, polymers, and biomass which possess large interlayer distance and disordered structure are the most widely investigated anodes for SIBs, but the reversible capacities are usually in 150–300 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> with low initial Coulombic efficiency of 20–50%. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref21 ref22 ref23'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21 ref22 ref23" aria-label="reference 21 to 23">(21-23)</a> Considering the capacity and Coulombic efficiency, transition metal oxides (M<sub><i>x</i></sub>O<sub><i>y</i></sub>) (M = Co, Ni, Cu, Fe, Mo) which have been used as LIBs anode materials based on the conversion reaction mechanism might be the most potential candidates for the SIBs anodes because of the analogous sodium storage mechanism. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref8 ref9 ref10'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8 ref9 ref10" aria-label="reference 8 to 10">(8-10)</a> Iron oxides (Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>), with high capacity and low cost, has recently attracted many researchers as a promising anode material for SIBs. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref24 ref25'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24 ref25" aria-label="reference 24 and 25">(24, 25)</a> Electrochemical performances of Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> in SIBs was first reported by Komaba et al. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref26'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26" aria-label="reference 26">(26)</a> This work demonstrated the electrochemical activity of Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> in relation to the particle size effect, whose electrochemical activity significantly enhances as the mean particle size decreases. Subsequently, Balaya et al. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref27'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27" aria-label="reference 27">(27)</a> demonstrated the conversion reaction of sodium storage in Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> anode material, which exhibited an initial reversible capacity of 366 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 55.5 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. However, the practical application of Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> anode material was restricted by the low electronic conductivity and serious volume change during charging–discharging process resulting in poor cycle stability and unsatisfactory rate performance. Although various effective strategies have been proposed to solve these problems, such as reducing particle size, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref28'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28" aria-label="reference 28">(28)</a> coating with carbon materials, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref29 ref30'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29 ref30" aria-label="reference 29 and 30">(29, 30)</a> encapsulated in a carbonaceous matrix, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref31 ref32'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31 ref32" aria-label="reference 31 and 32">(31, 32)</a> and choosing appropriate binders, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref25'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25" aria-label="reference 25">(25)</a> Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>-based anode with excellent electrochemical performance for sodium ion batteries remains a great challenge.</div><div class="NLM_p last">Herein, an effective strategy to in situ fabricate a 3D-0D graphene-Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> quantum dot hybrids (QDs) by a facile hydrothermal approach as anode materials was proposed for high performance SIBs. In the hybrids, Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> QDs are 0D nanocrystals with an average size of 4.9 nm, which fixed on the surface of 3D structured graphene nanosheets homogeneously. The ultrafine particle size of Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> QDs is beneficial to enhance their electrochemical activity and curtail the diffusion length of sodium ions, while the graphene provides a good 3D interconnected network for fast diffusion of electrons and rapid mass transport, as well as effectively prevent aggregation of Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> and accommodate their volume changes during charging–discharging process. These unique features endow the hybrid 3D-0D graphene-Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> QDs with ultrahigh sodium storage capacity, excellent cycling stability and superior rate performance, implying one of the most promising sodium storage materials.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">2 Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i3" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i3">2.1 Preparation of the 3D-0D Graphene-Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> QD Hybrids</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Graphene oxide (GO) were prepared with a modified Hummer’s method as reported previously. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref33'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33" aria-label="reference 33">(33)</a> The 3D-0D graphene-Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> QD hybrids were prepared through a facile hydrothermal treatment of graphene oxide and ferric citrate and subsequent annealing treatment in N<sub>2</sub> atomsphere. Specifically, 200 mg of GO dissolved in 80 mL of deionized water was sonicated for 2 h, and then 1.5 mmol of ferric citrate (FeC<sub>6</sub>H<sub>5</sub>O<sub>7</sub> 5 H<sub>2</sub>O; Aladdin) was directly added into the GO solution and stirred at 80 °C for 1 h. The resulting solution was transferred into a Teflon-line stainless steel autoclave and heated at 180 °C for 12 h. Subsequently, the product was centrifuged and washed in deionized water followed by freeze-drying. The resulting powder was heated in a quartz tube to 500 °C at a rate of 5 °C min<sup>–1</sup> in N<sub>2</sub> atmosphere and kept at 500 °C for 2 h. For comparison, pure Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> nanoparticles were prepared in the similar conditions only besides GO addition.</div></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i4" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i4">2.2 Morphology and Structure Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The morphology features were studied with scanning electron microscopy (SEM, Hitachis4800) and high-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM, JEOL-2100). The crystal structure of the samples was investigated by X-ray diffraction (XRD) using Cu Kα radiation. The Raman spectrum was measured at 514 nm excitation by Renishaw 1000 Raman spectrometer. Nitrogen adsorption/desorption measurements were carried out with ASAP 2460 (Micromeritics) to characterize porosity properties of the samples. Before the test, the samples were treated under vacuum at 300 °C for about 12 h to outgas until the pressure decreased below 5 μmHg. The specific surface area (<i>S</i><sub>BET</sub>) was calculated according to the conventional BET (Brunauer–Emmett–Teller) method, the total pore volume (<i>V</i><sub>t</sub>) was calculated from the amount of the adsorbed N<sub>2</sub> at a relative pressure of 0.99, and the pore size distribution was obtained based on the density function theory (DFT) method with a carbon slit pore equilibrium model. Thermal gravimetric analysis (TGA; DTG-60A) was carried out under air atmosphere with a heating rate of 10 °C min<sup>–1</sup>.</div></div><div id="sec2_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i5">2.3 Electrochemical Measurement</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The working electrodes were prepared as follows. The active material, conductivity agent (super-P) and binder (sodium alginate) were mixed with a ratio of 80:10:10 in mass with <i>N</i>-methylpyrrolidone as solvent. Electrodes were prepared by coating the mixed slurry on a copper foil, drying under vacuum at 120 °C for 12 h and then cutting into pellets with a diameter of 10 mm. The mass loading of the active materials in each electrode was around 0.9 mg cm<sup>–2</sup>. Coin cells (CR2025) were assembled under Ar atmosphere with sodium metal as the counter electrode, glass fiber as the separator, and NaClO<sub>4</sub> (1 M) in ethylene carbonate (EC) and diethyl carbonate (DEC) (1:1 by volume) as the electrolyte. Cyclic voltammetry (CV) measurement was performed at a rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup> in the voltage range of 0.001–3.0 V on a CS350 electrochemical workstation. The cycling stability and rate performance of the cells were tested between 0.001 and 3.0 V (vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na) with a battery testing system (Land BT2000). The specific capacity was calculated based on the total amount of the graphene-Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> hybrids mass in the electrodes.</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i6">3 Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">The 3D-0D graphene-Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> QD hybrids were synthesized by hydrothermal treatment of graphene oxide and ferric citrate and subsequent annealing in N<sub>2</sub> as illustrated in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>. First, ferric citrate (FeC<sub>6</sub>H<sub>5</sub>O<sub>7</sub>) was mixed with GO solution and stirred at 80 °C. In this step, the functional groups on the surface and edge of GO sheets like carboxyl, hydroxyl, and epoxy groups can fully react with the iron ions and hydroxyl in FeC<sub>6</sub>H<sub>5</sub>O<sub>7</sub>. Consequently, ferric citrate would slowly hydrolyze, and the resulting products can be in situ tightly absorbed on the GO sheets. Subsequently, the hydrothermal process was performed. At this point, GO was partially reduced and assembled to a 3D structured hydrogel, and the absorbed Fe<sup>3+</sup> were partly reduced to Fe<sup>2+</sup>. Finally, annealing treatment was performed for increasing the crystallization of Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> and reducing the GO to graphene. Thus, Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> QDs were fixed on the surface of 3D structured graphene nanosheets uniformly, generating the designed 3D-0D graphene-Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> QD hybrids (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>).</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b09496/asset/images/medium/am-2016-09496z_0008.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b09496/asset/images/large/am-2016-09496z_0008.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. Scheme of the synthetic process of the hybrid 3D-0D graphene-Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> QDs.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b09496/asset/images/large/am-2016-09496z_0008.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.6b09496&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The SEM and TEM were carried out for the morphological and structure information (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>). <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a shows TEM image of the pure Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>, whose serious aggregation can greatly degrade the electrochemical performance. The graphene-Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> hybrids show different morphology from the pure Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>. As shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a> and <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>b, the graphene nanosheets are assembled to macroscopic 3D structure during hydrothermal process, similar to the reported graphene hydrogels. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref34'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34" aria-label="reference 34">(34)</a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>c,d indicates ultrafine Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> QDs without aggregation are uniformly anchored on the surface of graphene nanosheets, which could provide space for elastic buffer in order to prevent aggregation of Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> QDs and accommodate their volume change during the charging–discharging process. The broad rings in the electron diffraction pattern (inset of <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>d) implies the poor crystallinity of the Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> in the graphene-Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> hybrids. <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>f confirms the evenly distribution and uniform particle size of the Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> QDs with an average particle size of 4.9 nm, as evidenced by the corresponding size distribution analysis (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>e). The ultrafine particles of Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> QDs is beneficial for improving the electrochemical activity of the hybrids.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b09496/asset/images/medium/am-2016-09496z_0009.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b09496/asset/images/large/am-2016-09496z_0009.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. TEM image of the pure Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> (a), SEM images of the graphene-Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> hybrids (b, c), HRTEM images of graphene-Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> hybrids (d, f), and the particle size distribution of the Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> QDs (e).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b09496/asset/images/large/am-2016-09496z_0009.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.6b09496&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">As shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a, the XRD patterns of the graphene-Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> hybrids are in accordance with those of Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> (Magnetite, JCPDS card no. 19-0629). It is shown in the Rietveld refinement of the XRD that a crystalline Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> exhibits inverse spinel structure based on <i>Fd</i>3<i>m</i> space group (insert of <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a). The graphene-Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> hybrids are studied by Raman spectroscopy to investigate the structural transformation of the carbon framework in the reaction process from GO to rGO. As shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>b, two prominent peaks can be observed in the typical Raman spectrum of GO at 1599 and 1356 cm<sup>–1</sup> corresponding to the G and D bands, respectively. The order degree of the crystal structure of carbon materials is evaluated by the intensity ratio of D to G band (<i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub>). The <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> of the GO and the graphene-Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> hybrids are 0.9 and 0.95, respectively, indicating that in situ reduction has occurred in the thermal treatment process of GO. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref35 ref36'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35 ref36" aria-label="reference 35 and 36">(35, 36)</a></div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b09496/asset/images/medium/am-2016-09496z_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b09496/asset/images/large/am-2016-09496z_0001.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. XRD patterns (a), Raman spectra (b), N<sub>2</sub> sorption isotherms (c), DFT pore size distribution (d), XPS (e), and TGA (f) curves of the samples.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b09496/asset/images/large/am-2016-09496z_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.6b09496&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Nitrogen sorption measurements were performed to investigate the porous properties of the samples. As shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>c, the adsorption/desorption isotherm of 3D-0D graphene-Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> QD hybrids is typically a type IV isotherm in which the distinct hysteresis loop is at a relative pressure (<i>p</i>/<i>p</i><sub>0</sub>) in range of 0.4–1, indicating the existence of abundant mesopores. The 3D-0D graphene-Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> QD hybrids has a BET specific surface area up to 317 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup> with a pore volume of 0.359 cm<sup>3</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>, much higher than those of pure Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> (27.4 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup> and 0.170 cm<sup>3</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>) and other graphene-based hybrids reported previously. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref37 ref38 ref39'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37 ref38 ref39" aria-label="reference 37 to 39">(37-39)</a> The DFT pore size distribution (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>d) of the graphene-Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> hybrids is composed of a few micropores of ∼1–2 nm and abundant mesopores in a wide size range between 2 and 50 nm, proving the highly mesoporous structure. Such highly developed porosity may be originated from the external surface of both graphene nanosheets and ultrafine Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> QDs, as well as the void spaces in the 3D structure of the hybrids. The large surface area and abundant porosity is beneficial for accelerating mass diffusion of electrolyte and accommodating volume changes of the Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> QDs during charge/discharge, and thus much improved sodium storage performances can be expected. The existence of C, Fe, and O elements is displayed by the XPS spectrum of graphene-Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> hybrids in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>e. The high-resolution XPS spectrum (inset of <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>e) for Fe 2p is a typical spectrum of Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> with broad peaks around 710.8 and 724.2 eV relating to Fe 2p<sup>3/2</sup> and Fe 2p<sup>1/2</sup> states, respectively. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref40'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40" aria-label="reference 40">(40)</a> The mass content of the graphene in the graphene-Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> is evaluated to be 49.95% by TGA (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>f).</div><div class="NLM_p">The integration of the 0D Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> QDs and the 3D graphene conducting network should lead to excellent electrochemical sodium storage performances of the 3D-0D graphene-Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> QD hybrids. Cyclic voltammetry (CV) was performed to characterize the Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction behavior of the pure Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a) and graphene-Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> hybrids (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>b). <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a shows the initial three cyclic voltammograms of the pure Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> electrode. The broad peak centered at ∼0.75 V in the first cathodic scan can be attributed to the structural transition induced by intercalation of the sodium into the crystalline Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>. In addition, the cathodic peak at ∼0.04 V should correspond to the complete reduction of Fe<sup>2+/3+</sup> to Fe<sup>0</sup> and the Na<sub>2</sub>O was formed by conversion reaction, as illustrated in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>e. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref27'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27" aria-label="reference 27">(27)</a> Because of the full conversion process, the peak in the first reduction process is very strong sharp in the CV curve of the sodium ion cell. In the anodic scan, a wide peak appears at ∼1.5 V, corresponding to the reoxidation process of Fe to Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> and decomposition of Na<sub>2</sub>O. Theoretically, the formation of Fe and Na<sub>2</sub>O as well as the reformation of Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> can be expressed by the electrochemical reaction as follows: Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> + 8Na<sup>+</sup> + 8e<sup>–</sup> ↔ 4Na<sub>2</sub>O + 3Fe. Moreover, the continuously decreased current of both the anodic and cathodic peaks during the initial three CV scans implies the poor electrochemical stability of the pure Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>. In contrast, the 3D-0D graphene-Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> QD hybrids exhibit a reversible and stable insertion/extraction behavior as shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>b. The intense irreversible peaks occur below 0.8 V vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup> in the first cathodic scan, which is attributed to the electrolyte decomposition and the formation of solid electrolyte interface (SEI) layer. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref42'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42" aria-label="reference 42">(42)</a> A couple of broad peaks locate at around 0.75 V (cathodic) and 1.0 V (anodic), which can be attributed to the Na<sup>+</sup> insertion into/extraction from the crystalline Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>. The CV curves in the following scan cycles overlap well, indicating good reversibility of the graphene-Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> hybrids.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b09496/asset/images/medium/am-2016-09496z_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b09496/asset/images/large/am-2016-09496z_0002.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. CV curves at 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup> for the pure Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> (a) and the hybrid 3D-0D graphene-Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>QDs (b), discharge–charge curves of the pure Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> (c) and the hybrid 3D-0D graphene-Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>QDs (d) at 30 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, and scheme of the electrochemical process of Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> anode electrodes (e).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b09496/asset/images/large/am-2016-09496z_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.6b09496&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p"><a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>c,d shows the charge–discharge profiles of the pure Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> and the 3D-0D graphene-Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> QD hybrids at 30 mA g<sup>–1</sup> in the voltage range between 0.001 and 3.0 V. The graphene-Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> hybrids exhibit long sloping charge–discharge profile with no apparent plateau (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>d). Considering the Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> QDs anchored on the surface of 3D structured graphene have ultrafine particle size of ∼4.9 nm and the hybrids has a high surface area, it is assumed that pseudocapacitance originated from surface fast sodiation/desodiation reaction and double layer capacitance based on electrostatic adsorption may give some contribution to the total sodium storage capacity, benefiting the rate performance of the hybrids. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref40 ref41'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40 ref41" aria-label="reference 40 and 41">(40, 41)</a> The initial capacities of the 3D-0D graphene-Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> QD hybrids (based on the total amount of the graphene-Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> hybrids mass) in the discharging and charging processes are 779 and 525 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively, much higher than those of the pure Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> (416 and 311 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>), indicating the improved electrochemical activity of the graphene-Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> hybrids. As the capacity of pure graphene is 237.3 mAh/g at 30 mA g<sup>–1</sup> (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.6b09496/suppl_file/am6b09496_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>) and occupies the mass ratio of 49.95% in the hybrids, the capacity contribution of Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>QDs is estimated to be 812 mAh/g, which is a very high value for Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>. In the subsequent cycles, compared with the pure Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>, the charge–discharge profiles of the graphene-Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> hybrids overlap well, indicating the improved reversibility. The reversible capacity of the graphene-Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> hybrids in the following cycles is almost stable which maintains at about 436 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after 10 cycles. Moreover, the Coulombic efficiency of the graphene-Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> hybrids substantially increases to ∼100%, which mainly results from the large electrochemically active surface area through the integration of the ultrafine Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> QDs and the graphene nanosheets.</div><div class="NLM_p">To further investigate the electrochemical characteristics of the graphene-Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> hybrids, cycling stability test was carried out at 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup> for 200 cycles. As shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a, the graphene-Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> hybrids exhibit much improved cycling performance than the pure Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>. The measured initial discharging and charging capacities of the graphene-Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> hybrids at 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup> are 659 and 440 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. After 200 cycles, the graphene-Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> hybrids can deliver a reversible capacity of 312 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> with the Coulombic efficiency of ∼99.7%, which is ∼71% of the initial reversible capacity, while the capacity of pure Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> decreases to only 23 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>. <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>b shows the cycling stability of the graphene-Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> hybrids at a current density of 500 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. After 200 cycles, a stable charge capacity of ∼220 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> remains. TEM observation shows the Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> QDs are still uniformly anchored on the graphene nanosheets after 200 cycles (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>d), confirming that the graphene-Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> hybrids can maintain its original morphology and hierarchical structure after long discharge–charge cycles. The outstanding cycle performance can be ascribed to the unique 3D-0D hybrid nanostructure. The size effect of ultrafine Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> QDs could dramatically mitigate the volume changes during charge–discharge, which could be easily accommodated by the 3D structured graphene nanosheets, so the hybrids can maintain the structural integrity and prevent particle pulverization upon cycling.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b09496/asset/images/medium/am-2016-09496z_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b09496/asset/images/large/am-2016-09496z_0003.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. (a) Cyclic performance of the hybrid 3D-0D graphene-Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> QDs and pure Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> at 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, (b) cyclic performance of the hybrid 3D-0D graphene-Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> QDs at 500 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, (c) comparison of rate capacity of the hybrid 3D-0D graphene-Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> QDs in this work and other electrodes based on transition-metal oxide reported for SIBs, (d) TEM image of the hybrid 3D-0D graphene-Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> QDs after 200 cycles.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b09496/asset/images/large/am-2016-09496z_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.6b09496&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The rate capability of the 3D-0D graphene-Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>QD hybrids was studied at various current densities from 30 mA g<sup>–1</sup> to 10 A g<sup>–1</sup>, as shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig6" aria-label="Figure 6">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>a. Pure Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> shows poor rate performance with capacities of only 45 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup> and 18 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.5 A g<sup>–1</sup>. In contrast, the capacities of the graphene-Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> hybrids at 100, 200, 500, 1000, 2000, and 5000 mA g<sup>–1</sup> reach 316, 273, 216, 164, 113, and 62 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. Even when cycled at a high current density of 10 A g<sup>–1</sup>, the electrode still maintains a reversible capacity of 56 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>. In addition, when the discharge current density decreases back to 30 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, the reversible capacity increases to 411 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>. Notably, the rate capability of the graphene-Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> is super to the previously reported Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>-based anodes including spinel Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref25'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25" aria-label="reference 25">(25)</a> Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>-rGO, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref29'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29" aria-label="reference 29">(29)</a> Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>-porous carbon, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref31'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31" aria-label="reference 31">(31)</a> and C/Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>-CNTs composites, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref43 ref44'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43 ref44" aria-label="reference 43 and 44">(43, 44)</a> as well as among the best anode materials with high rate capability of other transition-metal oxide (such as Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> and TiO<sub>2</sub>)-based anodes reported recently, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref45 ref46'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref45 ref46" aria-label="reference 45 and 46">(45, 46)</a> as shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>c.</div><figure id="fig6" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b09496/asset/images/medium/am-2016-09496z_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b09496/asset/images/large/am-2016-09496z_0004.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. Rate performance (a) and Nyquist plots (b) of the hybrid 3D-0D graphene-Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>QDs and the pure Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> and magnified Nyquist plots of the hybrid 3D-0D graphene-Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>QDs (c) with the equivalent circuit in the inset.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b09496/asset/images/large/am-2016-09496z_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.6b09496&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">We experimentally observed that the hybrid 3D-0D graphene-Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> QDs exhibit much enhanced electrochemical performance as anodes for SIBs compared to the pure Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> nanoparticles. To further clarify the improved electrochemical performance of the graphene-Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> hybrids, electrochemical impedance spectroscopy was performed in the frequency range of 100–0.01 Hz. <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig6" aria-label="Figure 6">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>b shows the Nyquist plots of the graphene-Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> hybrids and the pure Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> nanoparticles in the fresh coin cells. The two samples both have the depressed semicircle in the medium-frequency followed by a linear tail in the low-frequency but exhibit much different profiles. <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig6" aria-label="Figure 6">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>c shows the detailed impedance spectra of the graphene-Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> hybrids, and the inset indicates the equivalent circuit with good fit for the EIS spectra of the graphene-Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> hybrids. The values of <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> representing the charge transfer resistance for the graphene-Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> hybrids and pure Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> electrode are 362 Ω and 2.44 × 10<sup>4</sup> Ω, respectively. This indicates that the graphene-Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> hybrids possess much higher charge transfer efficiency and the consequent rapid electron transport in the Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction process. As a result, the rate performance improves significantly. In addition, the inclined line in the low-frequency region means the Warburg impedance (<i>Z</i><sub>w</sub>) corresponding to the sodium ion diffusion in the solid. The much steeper straight line suggests faster sodium ion kinetics in the graphene-Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> hybrid electrode than in the pure Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> electrode, thanks to the highly developed 3D porous structure of the graphene-Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> hybrids.</div><div class="NLM_p">The excellent electrochemical performance of the hybrid 3D-0D graphene-Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> QDs as anodes for SIBs might result from the synergetic effect of the 0D Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> QDs and 3D conductive network of graphene nanosheets as illustrated in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig7" aria-label="Figure 7">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>: (i) the ultrafine Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> QDs and the covalent anchoring on the graphene substrates could provide large electrode/electrolyte interface area and inhibit particle aggregation, resulting in high reversible capacity. (ii) The ultrafine particle size of Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> QDs could dramatically mitigate the volume changes during conversion process, which can be easily accommodate by the buffer, 3D structured graphene nanosheets, guaranteeing the structural integrity during charge/discharge and thus excellent cycling stability, (iii) the ultrafine Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> QDs drastically shorten the diffusion path of sodium ion and electrons which can effectively and quickly transfer between the Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> QDs and the current collector based on graphene nanosheets with high conductivity, while the 3D porous structure facilitates the easy access and fast diffusion of the electrolytes, enable the hybrids possesses superior rate capability.</div><figure id="fig7" data-index="7" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 7</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b09496/asset/images/medium/am-2016-09496z_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b09496/asset/images/large/am-2016-09496z_0005.jpeg" id="gr7" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 7. Synergistic electrochemical characteristics of the hybrid graphene-Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> QDs.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b09496/asset/images/large/am-2016-09496z_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.6b09496&amp;id=fig7"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i7">4 Conclusion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">A facile hydrothermal approach was proposed to in situ anchor 0D Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> QDs on the surface of 3D structured graphene nanosheets for fabricating 3D-0D graphene-Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> QD hybrids as anodes for SIBs. Benefiting from the synergetic effect of the 0D Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> QDs and 3D conductive network of graphene nanosheets, the hybrid 3D-0D graphene-Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> QDs exhibit an ultrahigh sodium storage capacity of 525 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 30 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, outstanding cycle stability (312 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after 200 cycles at 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup>), and superior rate performance (56 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 10 A g<sup>–1</sup>), making it a very attractive anode material for SIBs. Furthermore, the unique hierarchical structure of these hybrid 3D-0D graphene-Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> QDs are expected to find widespread application in other energy storage devices like lithium ion batteries and supercapacitors. Inspired by this strategy, some other hybrid 3D-0D graphene-metal oxide quantum dots with hierarchical structure can be also synthesized.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes-1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i8">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge on the <a href="/" class="ext-link">ACS Publications website</a> at DOI: <a href="/doi/abs/10.1021/acsami.6b09496" class="ext-link">10.1021/acsami.6b09496</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Rate capability of the rGO electrode and cycling performance of the rGO electrode (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.6b09496/suppl_file/am6b09496_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.6b09496/suppl_file/am6b09496_si_001.pdf">am6b09496_si_001.pdf (259.7 kb)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.6b09496" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget" style="display: block;"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">
